<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox references</a>
</td></tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;">
</td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p><b>Sulfuric acid</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sulfur#Spelling_and_etymology" title="Sulfur">alternative spelling</a> <b>sulphuric acid</b>), also known as <b><a href="/wiki/Vitriol" title="Vitriol">vitriol</a></b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Mineral_acid" title="Mineral acid">mineral acid</a> composed of the elements <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_formula" title="Molecular formula">molecular formula</a> <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">H<sub>2</sub></a><a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">SO<sub>4</sub></a>. It is a colorless, odorless, and syrupy liquid that is soluble in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, in a reaction that is highly exothermic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ds-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Its corrosiveness can be mainly ascribed to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_acid" title="Strong acid">strong acidic nature</a>, and, if at a high concentration, its <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydrating</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizing" title="Oxidizing">oxidizing</a> properties. It is also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a>, readily absorbing <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air" title="Air">air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ds-4">[4]</a></sup> Upon contact, sulfuric acid can cause severe <a href="/wiki/Chemical_burn" title="Chemical burn">chemical burns</a> and even secondary <a href="/wiki/Burn" title="Burn">thermal burns</a>; it is very dangerous even at moderate <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentrations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OA_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-OA-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TB_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-TB-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfuric acid is a very important commodity chemical, and indeed, a nation's sulfuric acid production is a good indicator of its industrial strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> It is widely produced with different methods, such as <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wet_sulfuric_acid_process" title="Wet sulfuric acid process">wet sulfuric acid process</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a> and some other methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common use of sulfuric acid (60% of total) is for fertilizer manufacture.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It is also a central substance in the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a>. Principal uses include <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> manufacturing (and other <a href="/wiki/Mineral_processing" title="Mineral processing">mineral processing</a>), <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refining</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wastewater_processing" title="Wastewater processing">wastewater processing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a>. It has a wide range of end applications including in <a href="/wiki/Drain_cleaner" title="Drain cleaner">domestic acidic drain cleaners</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dc_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-dc-9">[9]</a></sup> as an <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead-acid batteries</a> and in various <a href="/wiki/Cleaning_agent" title="Cleaning agent">cleaning agents</a>.
</p>
<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grades_of_sulfuric_acid">Grades of sulfuric acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Grades of sulfuric acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although nearly 100% sulfuric acid can be made, the subsequent loss of <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></a></span> at the boiling point brings the concentration to 98.3% acid. The 98% grade is more stable in storage, and is the usual form of what is described as "concentrated sulfuric acid". Other concentrations are used for different purposes. Some common concentrations are:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB11_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB11-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Mass fraction<br/>H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>
</th>
<th>Density<br/>(kg/L)
</th>
<th>Concentration<br/>(mol/L)
</th>
<th>Common name
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>10%</td>
<td>1.07</td>
<td align="center">≈1</td>
<td>dilute sulfuric acid
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>29–32%</td>
<td>1.25–1.28</td>
<td align="center">4.2–5</td>
<td>battery acid<br/>(used in <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead–acid batteries</a>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>62–70%</td>
<td>1.52–1.60</td>
<td align="center">9.6–11.5</td>
<td>chamber acid<br/>fertilizer acid
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>78–80%</td>
<td>1.70–1.73</td>
<td align="center">13.5–14</td>
<td>tower acid<br/>Glover acid
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>98%</td>
<td>1.84</td>
<td align="center">≈18</td>
<td>concentrated sulfuric acid
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>"Chamber acid" and "tower acid" were the two concentrations of sulfuric acid produced by the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a>, chamber acid being the acid produced in the lead chamber itself (&lt;70% to avoid contamination with <a href="/wiki/Nitrosylsulfuric_acid" title="Nitrosylsulfuric acid">nitrosylsulfuric acid</a>) and tower acid being the acid recovered from the bottom of the Glover tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB11_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB11-11">[11]</a></sup> They are now obsolete as commercial concentrations of sulfuric acid, although they may be prepared in the laboratory from concentrated sulfuric acid if needed. In particular, "10M" sulfuric acid (the modern equivalent of chamber acid, used in many <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">titrations</a>) is prepared by slowly adding 98% sulfuric acid to an equal volume of water, with good stirring: the temperature of the mixture can rise to 80 °C (176 °F) or higher.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB11_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB11-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfuric acid reacts with its anhydride, <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, to form <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span>, called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrosulfuric_acid" title="Pyrosulfuric acid">pyrosulfuric acid</a></i>, <i>fuming sulfuric acid</i>, <i>Disulfuric acid</i> or <i>oleum</i> or, less commonly, <i>Nordhausen acid</i>. Concentrations of oleum are either expressed in terms of % <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> (called % oleum) or as % <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> (the amount made if <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> were added); common concentrations are 40% oleum (109% <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) and 65% oleum (114.6% <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>). Pure <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span></span> is a solid with melting point of 36 °C.
</p><p>Pure sulfuric acid has a vapor pressure of &lt;0.001 mmHg at 25 °C and 1 mmHg at 145.8 °C,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OEHHA_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-OEHHA-12">[12]</a></sup> and 98% sulfuric acid has a &lt;1 mmHg vapor pressure at 40 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhodia_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhodia-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pure sulfuric acid is a viscous clear liquid, like oil, and this explains the old name of the acid ('oil of vitriol').
</p><p>Commercial sulfuric acid is sold in several different purity grades. Technical grade <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is impure and often colored, but is suitable for making fertilizer. Pure grades, such as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Pharmacopeia" title="United States Pharmacopeia">United States Pharmacopeia</a> (USP) grade, are used for making <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceuticals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyestuffs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Analytical_chemistry" title="Analytical chemistry">Analytical</a> grades are also available.
</p><p>Nine hydrates are known, but three of them were confirmed to be tetrahydrate (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O), hemihexahydrate (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>·​<span class="frac nowrap">6<span class="visualhide"> </span><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>2</sub></span>H<sub>2</sub>O) and octahydrate (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>·8H<sub>2</sub>O).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polarity_and_conductivity">Polarity and conductivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Polarity and conductivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<tr>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>
</td>
<td>0.1

<p><a href="/wiki/Anhydrous" title="Anhydrous">Anhydrous</a> <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is a very <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> liquid, having a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">dielectric constant</a> of around 100. It has a high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, caused by dissociation through <a href="/wiki/Protonation" title="Protonation">protonating</a> itself, a process known as <a href="/wiki/Autoprotolysis" title="Autoprotolysis">autoprotolysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenwood_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_constant" title="Equilibrium constant">equilibrium constant</a> for the autoprotolysis is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenwood_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-greenwood-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><i>K</i><sub>ap</sub> (25 °C) = [<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>][<span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>] = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6996270000000000000♠</span>2.7<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−4</sup></span></dd></dl>
<p>The comparable <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">equilibrium constant for water</a>, <i>K</i><sub>w</sub> is 10<sup>−14</sup>, a factor of 10<sup>10</sup> (10 billion) smaller.
</p><p>In spite of the viscosity of the acid, the effective <a href="/wiki/Molar_conductivity" title="Molar conductivity">conductivities</a> of the <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> and <span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> ions are high due to an intramolecular proton-switch mechanism (analogous to the <a href="/wiki/Grotthuss_mechanism" title="Grotthuss mechanism">Grotthuss mechanism</a> in water), making sulfuric acid a good conductor of electricity. It is also an excellent solvent for many reactions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_properties">Chemical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Chemical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reaction_with_water_and_dehydrating_property">Reaction with water and dehydrating property</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reaction with water and dehydrating property">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG/220px-Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG/330px-Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG/440px-Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulphuric_acid_on_a_piece_of_towel.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drops of concentrated sulfuric acid rapidly decompose a piece of cotton towel by dehydration.</div></div></div>

When concentrated sulfuric acid comes into contact with <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, slow carbonification of the sucrose takes place. The reaction is accompanied by the evolution of gaseous products that contribute to the formation of the foamy carbon pillar that rises above the beaker.</div></div></div>
<p>Because the <a href="/wiki/Hydration_reaction" title="Hydration reaction">hydration reaction</a> of sulfuric acid is highly <a href="/wiki/Exothermic_reaction" title="Exothermic reaction">exothermic</a>, dilution should always be performed by adding the acid to the <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">water</a> rather than the water to the acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Because the reaction is in an equilibrium that favors the rapid protonation of water, addition of acid to the water ensures that the <i>acid</i> is the limiting reagent. This reaction is best thought of as the formation of <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ions:
</p>

style="padding-left:2em;"> </span> <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7006240000000000000♠</span>2.4<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>6</sup></span> (strong acid)</dd>
<dd><span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> <span style="padding-left:2em;"> </span> <i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998100000000000000♠</span>1.0<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−2</sup></span> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><span class="chemf nowrap">HSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bisulfate" title="Bisulfate">bisulfate</a></i> anion and <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is the <i><a href="/wiki/Sulfate" title="Sulfate">sulfate</a></i> anion. <i>K</i><sub>a1</sub> and <i>K</i><sub>a2</sub> are the <a href="/wiki/Acid_dissociation_constant" title="Acid dissociation constant">acid dissociation constants</a>.
</p><p>Because the hydration of sulfuric acid is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermodynamic" title="Thermodynamic">thermodynamically</a> favorable and the affinity of it for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" title="Water (molecule)">water</a> is sufficiently strong, sulfuric acid is an excellent dehydrating agent. Concentrated sulfuric acid has a very powerful <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydrating</a> property, removing water (<a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">H<sub>2</sub>O</a>) from other <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> including <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and producing <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, heat, steam.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a>, this is often demonstrated by mixing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Table_sugar" title="Table sugar">table sugar</a> (sucrose) into sulfuric acid. The sugar changes from white to dark brown and then to black as carbon is formed. A rigid column of black, porous carbon will emerge as well. The carbon will smell strongly of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caramel_(aroma)" title="Caramel (aroma)">caramel</a> due to the heat generated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \overbrace {{\ce {C12H22O11}}} ^{\text{sucrose}}\ {\ce {-&gt;[{\ce {H2SO4}}]}}\ {\underset {\text{(black graphitic foam)}}{{\ce {12C}}}}+{\ce {11H2O}}_{\text{(g,l)}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>Similarly, mixing <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> into concentrated sulfuric acid will give elemental <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> and water as absorbed by the sulfuric acid (which becomes slightly diluted). The effect of this can be seen when concentrated sulfuric acid is spilled on paper which is composed of <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>; the cellulose reacts to give a <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">burnt</a> appearance, the <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> appears much as soot would in a fire.
Although less dramatic, the action of the acid on <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, even in diluted form, will destroy the fabric.
</p>
