</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {{\overset {fluorapatite}{Ca5F(PO4)3}}+{5H2SO4}+10H2O-&gt;{\overset {calcium~sulfate}{5CaSO4.2H2O}}+{HF}+3H3PO4}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/05d12461db2ed709a9e6d2030eb624dae321478c" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:73.43ex; height:5.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfate" title="Ammonium sulfate">Ammonium sulfate</a>, an important nitrogen fertilizer, is most commonly produced as a byproduct from <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coking plants</a> supplying the iron and steel making plants. Reacting the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> produced in the thermal decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> with waste sulfuric acid allows the ammonia to be crystallized out as a salt (often brown because of iron contamination) and sold into the agro-chemicals industry.
</p><p>Another important use for sulfuric acid is for the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">aluminium sulfate</a>, also known as paper maker's alum. This can react with small amounts of soap on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_pulp" title="Paper pulp">paper pulp</a> fibers to give gelatinous aluminium <a href="/wiki/Carboxylate" title="Carboxylate">carboxylates</a>, which help to coagulate the pulp fibers into a hard paper surface. It is also used for making <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_hydroxide" title="Aluminium hydroxide">aluminium hydroxide</a>, which is used at <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a> plants to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(water)" title="Filter (water)">filter</a> out impurities, as well as to improve the taste of the <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>. <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">Aluminium sulfate</a> is made by reacting <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a> with sulfuric acid:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">AlO(OH)</span> + 3 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → <span class="chemf nowrap">Al<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + 4 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span></dd></dl>
<p>Sulfuric acid is also important in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyestuffs</a> solutions.
</p>
<h3><span id="Sulfur.E2.80.93iodine_cycle"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sulfur–iodine_cycle">Sulfur–iodine cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sulfur–iodine cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sulfur%E2%80%93iodine_cycle" title="Sulfur–iodine cycle">sulfur–iodine cycle</a> is a series of thermo-chemical processes used to obtain <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>. It consists of three chemical reactions whose net reactant is <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> and whose net products are hydrogen and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>. Step one of cycle is the <a href="/wiki/Bunsen_reaction" title="Bunsen reaction">Bunsen reaction</a>.
</p>
<dl><dd><table>
<tbody><tr>
<td>2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> → 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></td>
<td>   </td>
<td>(830 °C)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> → 2 HI + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span></td>
<td>   </td>
<td>(120 °C)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2 HI → <span class="chemf nowrap">I<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span></td>
<td>   </td>
<td>(320 °C)
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<p>The sulfur and <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> compounds are recovered and reused, hence the consideration of the process as a cycle. This process is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> and must occur at high temperatures, so energy in the form of heat has to be supplied.
</p><p>The sulfur–iodine cycle has been proposed as a way to supply hydrogen for a <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_economy" title="Hydrogen economy">hydrogen-based economy</a>. It does not require <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> like current methods of <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">steam reforming</a>. But note that all of the available energy in the hydrogen so produced is supplied by the heat used to make it.
</p><p>The sulfur–iodine cycle is currently being researched as a feasible method of obtaining hydrogen, but the concentrated, corrosive acid at high temperatures poses currently insurmountable safety hazards if the process were built on a large scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_cleaning_agent">Industrial cleaning agent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Industrial cleaning agent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cleaning_agent" title="Cleaning agent">Cleaning agent</a></div>
<p>Sulfuric acid is used in large quantities by the <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steelmaking" title="Steelmaking">steelmaking</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel_industry" title="Steel industry">industry</a> to <a href="/wiki/Pickling_(metal)" title="Pickling (metal)">remove</a> oxidation, <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fouling" title="Fouling">scaling</a> from rolled sheet and billets prior to sale to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobile</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Major_appliances" title="Major appliances">major appliances</a> industry.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Used acid is often recycled using a spent acid regeneration (SAR) plant. These plants combust spent acid<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title='"What is it, exactly? Is it still the same acid, dirty, reacted, or what?" (February 2015)'>clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> with natural gas, refinery gas, fuel oil or other fuel sources. This combustion process produces gaseous <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>) which are then used to manufacture "new" sulfuric acid. SAR plants are common additions to metal smelting plants, oil refineries, and other industries where sulfuric acid is consumed in bulk, as operating a SAR plant is much cheaper than the recurring costs of spent acid disposal and new acid purchases.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">Hydrogen peroxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) can be added to sulfuric acid to produce <a href="/wiki/Piranha_solution" title="Piranha solution">piranha solution</a>, a powerful but very toxic cleaning solution with which substrate surfaces can be cleaned. Piranha solution is typically used in the microelectronics industry, and also in laboratory settings to clean glassware.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catalyst">Catalyst</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Catalyst">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfuric acid is used for a variety of other purposes in the chemical industry. For example, it is the usual acid catalyst for the conversion of <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexanone_oxime" title="Cyclohexanone oxime">cyclohexanone oxime</a> to <a href="/wiki/Caprolactam" title="Caprolactam">caprolactam</a>, used for making <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">nylon</a>. It is used for making <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> from <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_process" title="Mannheim process">Mannheim process</a>. Much <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> is used in <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> refining, for example as a catalyst for the reaction of <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a> with <a href="/wiki/Isobutylene" title="Isobutylene">isobutylene</a> to give <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isooctane" title="Isooctane">isooctane</a>, a compound that raises the <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> (petrol). Sulfuric acid is also often used as a dehydrating or oxidising agent in industrial reactions, such as the dehydration of various sugars to form solid carbon.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrolyte">Electrolyte</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Electrolyte">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_(sulphuric_acid).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg/220px-Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg/330px-Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg/440px-Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_%28sulphuric_acid%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acidic_drain_cleaner_containing_sulfuric_acid_(sulphuric_acid).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Acidic <a href="/wiki/Drain_cleaner" title="Drain cleaner">drain cleaners</a> usually contain sulfuric acid at a high concentration which turns a piece of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PH_paper" title="PH paper">pH paper</a> red and chars it instantly, demonstrating both the strong acidic nature and dehydrating property.</div></div></div>
<p>Sulfuric acid acts as the electrolyte in <a href="/wiki/Lead%E2%80%93acid_battery" title="Lead–acid battery">lead–acid batteries</a> (lead-acid accumulator):
</p><p>At <a href="/wiki/Anode" title="Anode">anode</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Pb</span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span><sup>2−</sup> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">PbSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 e<sup>−</sup></dd></dl>
<p>At <a href="/wiki/Cathode" title="Cathode">cathode</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">PbO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 H<sup>+</sup> + <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span><sup>2−</sup> + 2 e<sup>−</sup> ⇌ <span class="chemf nowrap">PbSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acidic_drain_opener.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acidic_drain_opener.JPG/220px-Acidic_drain_opener.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acidic_drain_opener.JPG/330px-Acidic_drain_opener.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Acidic_drain_opener.JPG/440px-Acidic_drain_opener.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acidic_drain_opener.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An acidic <a href="/wiki/Drain_cleaner" title="Drain cleaner">drain cleaner</a> can be used to dissolve grease, hair and even tissue paper inside water pipes.</div></div></div>
<p>Overall:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Pb</span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">PbO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 H<sup>+</sup> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span><sup>2−</sup> ⇌ 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">PbSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_uses">Domestic uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Domestic uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfuric acid at high concentrations is frequently the major ingredient in <a href="/wiki/Drain_cleaner#Acidic_drain_openers" title="Drain cleaner">acidic drain cleaners</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dc_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-dc-9">[9]</a></sup> which are used to remove <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">grease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hair" title="Hair">hair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tissue_paper" title="Tissue paper">tissue paper</a>, etc. Similar to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drain_opener" title="Drain opener">alkaline versions</a>, such drain openers can dissolve fats and proteins via <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a>. Moreover, as concentrated sulfuric acid has a strong dehydrating property, it can remove tissue paper via dehydrating process as well. Since the acid may react with water vigorously, such acidic drain openers should be added slowly into the pipe to be cleaned.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dalton%27s-sulphuric-acid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="98" data-file-width="309" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Dalton%27s-sulphuric-acid.jpg/220px-Dalton%27s-sulphuric-acid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Dalton%27s-sulphuric-acid.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dalton%27s-sulphuric-acid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Dalton" title="John Dalton">John Dalton</a>'s 1808 sulfuric acid molecule shows a central <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> atom bonded to three oxygen atoms, or <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anhydride" title="Anhydride">anhydride</a> of sulfuric acid.</div></div></div>
<p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Vitriol" title="Vitriol">vitriol</a>, a category of glassy minerals from which the acid can be derived, began in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">ancient times</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a> had a list of types of vitriol that they classified according to the substances' color. Some of the earliest discussions on the origin and properties of vitriol is in the works of the Greek physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioscorides" title="Dioscorides">Dioscorides</a> (first century AD) and the Roman naturalist <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> (23–79 AD). <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> also discussed its medical use. Metallurgical uses for vitriolic substances were recorded in the Hellenistic alchemical works of <a href="/wiki/Zosimos_of_Panopolis" title="Zosimos of Panopolis">Zosimos of Panopolis</a>, in the treatise <i>Phisica et Mystica</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Leyden_papyrus_X" title="Leyden papyrus X">Leyden papyrus X</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpenko_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karpenko-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alchemy_and_chemistry_in_medieval_Islam" title="Alchemy and chemistry in medieval Islam">Medieval Islamic era alchemists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C4%81bir_ibn_Hayy%C4%81n" title="Jābir ibn Hayyān">Jābir ibn Hayyān</a> (c. 721 – c. 815 AD, also known as Geber), <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Zakariya_al-Razi" title="Muhammad ibn Zakariya al-Razi">Razi</a> (865 – 925 AD), and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jamal_Din_al-Watwat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jamal Din al-Watwat (page does not exist)">Jamal Din al-Watwat</a> (d. 1318, wrote the book <i>Mabāhij al-fikar wa-manāhij al-'ibar</i>), included vitriol in their mineral classification lists. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Sina" title="Ibn Sina">Ibn Sina</a> focused on its medical uses and different varieties of vitriol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpenko_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karpenko-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfuric acid was called "oil of vitriol" by medieval European alchemists because it was prepared by roasting "green vitriol" (<a href="/wiki/Iron(II)_sulfate" title="Iron(II) sulfate">iron(II) sulfate</a>) in an iron <a href="/wiki/Retort" title="Retort">retort</a>. There are references to it in the works of <a href="/wiki/Vincent_of_Beauvais" title="Vincent of Beauvais">Vincent of Beauvais</a> and in the <i>Compositum de Compositis</i> ascribed to Saint <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a>. A passage from <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-Geber" title="Pseudo-Geber">Pseudo-Geber</a>´s <i>Summa Perfectionis</i> was long considered to be the first recipe for sulfuric acid, but this was a misinterpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpenko_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Karpenko-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the seventeenth century, the German-Dutch chemist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Glauber" title="Johann Glauber">Johann Glauber</a> prepared sulfuric acid by burning <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> together with <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">saltpeter</a> (<a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>, <span class="chemf nowrap">KNO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>), in the presence of steam. As saltpeter decomposes, it oxidizes the sulfur to <span class="chemf nowrap">SO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span>, which combines with water to produce sulfuric acid. In 1736, <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Ward" title="Joshua Ward">Joshua Ward</a>, a London pharmacist, used this method to begin the first large-scale production of sulfuric acid.
</p><p>In 1746 in Birmingham, <a href="/wiki/John_Roebuck" title="John Roebuck">John Roebuck</a> adapted this method to produce sulfuric acid in <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>-lined chambers, which were stronger, less expensive, and could be made larger than the previously used glass containers. This process allowed the effective industrialization of sulfuric acid production. After several refinements, this method, called the <a href="/wiki/Lead_chamber_process" title="Lead chamber process">lead chamber process</a> or "chamber process", remained the standard for sulfuric acid production for almost two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-b1-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfuric acid created by John Roebuck's process approached a 65% concentration. Later refinements to the lead chamber process by French chemist <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Louis_Gay-Lussac" title="Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac">Joseph Louis Gay-Lussac</a> and British chemist John Glover improved concentration to 78%. However, the manufacture of some <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a> and other chemical processes require a more concentrated product. Throughout the 18th century, this could only be made by <a href="/wiki/Dry_distillation" title="Dry distillation">dry distilling</a> minerals in a technique similar to the original <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemical</a> processes. <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">Pyrite</a> (iron disulfide, <span class="chemf nowrap">FeS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>) was heated in air to yield iron(II) sulfate, <span class="chemf nowrap">FeSO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>, which was oxidized by further heating in air to form <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_sulfate" title="Iron(III) sulfate">iron(III) sulfate</a>, Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, which, when heated to 480 °C, decomposed to <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron(III) oxide</a> and sulfur trioxide, which could be passed through water to yield sulfuric acid in any concentration. However, the expense of this process prevented the large-scale use of concentrated sulfuric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-b1-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1831, British <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a> merchant Peregrine Phillips patented the <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a>, which was a far more economical process for producing sulfur trioxide and concentrated sulfuric acid. Today, nearly all of the world's sulfuric acid is produced using this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-z1_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-z1-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_hazards">Laboratory hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Laboratory hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="1279" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg/220px-Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg/330px-Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg/440px-Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulfuric_acid_burning_tissue_paper.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Drops of 98% sulfuric acid char a piece of tissue paper instantly. Carbon is left after the dehydration reaction staining the paper black.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2488" data-file-width="2448" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg/220px-Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg/330px-Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg/440px-Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sulfuric_acid_98%25_chemical_burn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Superficial chemical burn caused by two 98% sulfuric acid splashes (forearm skin)</div></div></div>
<p>Sulfuric acid is capable of causing very severe burns, especially when it is at high <a href="/wiki/Concentration" title="Concentration">concentrations</a>. In common with other corrosive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">acids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>, it readily decomposes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteins" title="Proteins">proteins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">lipids</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amide_hydrolysis" title="Amide hydrolysis">amide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ester_hydrolysis" title="Ester hydrolysis">ester hydrolysis</a> upon contact with <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">living tissues</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">skin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flesh" title="Flesh">flesh</a>. In addition, it exhibits a strong <a href="/wiki/Dehydration_reaction" title="Dehydration reaction">dehydrating property</a> on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrates</a>, liberating extra <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> and causing <a href="/wiki/Burn#By_depth" title="Burn">secondary thermal burns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OA_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-OA-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TB_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-TB-6">[6]</a></sup> Accordingly, it rapidly attacks the <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a> and can induce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blindness" title="Blindness">permanent blindness</a> if splashed onto <a href="/wiki/Eye" title="Eye">eyes</a>. If ingested, it damages <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_organs" title="Internal organs">internal organs</a> irreversibly and may even be fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ds_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-ds-4">[4]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protective_equipment" title="Protective equipment">Protective equipment</a> should hence always be used when handling it. Moreover, its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidizing" title="Oxidizing">strong oxidizing property</a> makes it highly corrosive to many <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a> and may extend its destruction on other materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OA_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-OA-5">[5]</a></sup> Because of such reasons, damage posed by sulfuric acid is potentially more severe than that by other comparable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_acids" title="Strong acids">strong acids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrochloric_acid" title="Hydrochloric acid">hydrochloric acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a>.
</p>
<div style="float: right; margin-left: 1.0 em"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hazard_C.svg"><img alt="Hazard C.svg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Hazard_C.svg/70px-Hazard_C.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Hazard_C.svg/105px-Hazard_C.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Hazard_C.svg/140px-Hazard_C.svg.png 2x" width="70"/></a> <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dangclass8.png"><img alt="Dangclass8.png" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="432" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Dangclass8.png/70px-Dangclass8.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Dangclass8.png/105px-Dangclass8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Dangclass8.png/140px-Dangclass8.png 2x" width="70"/></a></div>
<p>Sulfuric acid must be stored carefully in containers made of nonreactive material (such as glass). Solutions equal to or stronger than 1.5 M are labeled "CORROSIVE", while solutions greater than 0.5 M but less than 1.5 M are labeled "IRRITANT". However, even the normal laboratory "dilute" grade (approximately 1 M, 10%) will char paper if left in contact for a sufficient time.
</p><p>The standard first aid treatment for acid spills on the skin is, as for other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosive" title="Corrosive">corrosive agents</a>, irrigation with large quantities of water. Washing is continued for at least ten to fifteen minutes to cool the tissue surrounding the acid burn and to prevent secondary damage. Contaminated clothing is removed immediately and the underlying skin washed thoroughly.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dilution_hazards">Dilution hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Dilution hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Preparation of the diluted acid can be dangerous due to the heat released in the dilution process. To avoid splattering, the concentrated acid is usually added to water and not the other way around. Water has a higher heat capacity than the acid, and so a vessel of cold water will absorb heat as acid is added.
</p>
<table class="wikitable floatleft">
<caption>Comparison of sulfuric acid and water
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Physical property
</th>
<th>H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>

<th>Water
</th>
<th>Units
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">Density</a>
</th>
<td>1.84
</td>
<td>1.0
</td>
<td>kg/L
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Volumetric_heat_capacity" title="Volumetric heat capacity">Volumetric heat capacity</a>
</th>
<td>2.54
</td>
<td>4.18
</td>
<td>kJ/L
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">Boiling point</a>
</th>
<td>337
</td>
<td>100
</td>
<td>°C
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Also, because the acid is denser than water, it sinks to the bottom. Heat is generated at the interface between acid and water, which is at the bottom of the vessel. Acid will not boil, because of its higher boiling point. Warm water near the interface rises due to <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>, which cools the interface, and prevents boiling of either acid or water.
</p><p>In contrast, addition of water to concentrated sulfuric acid results in a thin layer of water on top of the acid. Heat generated in this thin layer of water can boil, leading to the dispersal of a sulfuric acid <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosol</a> or worse, an <a href="/wiki/Explosion" title="Explosion">explosion</a>.
</p><p>Preparation of solutions greater than 6 M (35%) in concentration is most dangerous, because the heat produced may be sufficient to boil the diluted acid: efficient mechanical stirring and external cooling (such as an ice bath) are essential.
</p><p>Reaction rates double for about every 10 degree Celsius <a href="/wiki/Arrhenius_equation" title="Arrhenius equation">increase in temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Therefore, the reaction will become more violent as dilution proceeds, unless the mixture is given time to cool. Adding acid to warm water will cause a violent reaction.
</p><p>On a laboratory scale, sulfuric acid can be diluted by pouring concentrated acid onto crushed ice made from de-ionized water. The ice melts in an endothermic process while dissolving the acid. The amount of heat needed to melt the ice in this process is greater than the amount of heat evolved by dissolving the acid so the solution remains cold. After all the ice has melted, further dilution can take place using water.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_hazards">Industrial hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Industrial hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfuric acid is non-flammable.
</p><p>The main occupational risks posed by this acid are skin contact leading to burns (see above) and the inhalation of aerosols. Exposure to aerosols at high concentrations leads to immediate and severe irritation of the eyes, respiratory tract and mucous membranes: this ceases rapidly after exposure, although there is a risk of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_edema" title="Pulmonary edema">pulmonary edema</a> if tissue damage has been more severe. At lower concentrations, the most commonly reported symptom of chronic exposure to sulfuric acid aerosols is erosion of the teeth, found in virtually all studies: indications of possible chronic damage to the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory tract</a> are inconclusive as of 1997. Repeated occupational exposure to sulfuric acid mists may increase the chance of lung cancer by up to 64 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) for sulfuric acid is fixed at 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>: limits in other countries are similar. There have been reports of sulfuric acid ingestion leading to <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12_deficiency" title="Vitamin B12 deficiency">vitamin B12 deficiency</a> with subacute combined degeneration. The spinal cord is most often affected in such cases, but the optic nerves may show <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demyelination" title="Demyelination">demyelination</a>, loss of <a href="/wiki/Axon" title="Axon">axons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gliosis" title="Gliosis">gliosis</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_restrictions">Legal restrictions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfuric_acid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Legal restrictions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>International commerce of sulfuric acid is controlled under the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_Against_Illicit_Traffic_in_Narcotic_Drugs_and_Psychotropic_Substances" title="United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances">United Nations Convention Against Illicit Traffic in Narcotic Drugs and Psychotropic Substances, 1988</a>, which lists sulfuric acid under Table II of the convention as a chemical frequently used in the illicit manufacture of narcotic drugs or psychotropic substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incb_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-incb-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
